10.1016/j.jmp.2020.102435
Concerning the barrier provided by misconceptions to the learning of science, the  Committee on Undergraduate Science Education  wrote:  
                         Teachers can be astonished to learn that despite their best efforts, students do not grasp fundamental ideas covered in class. Even some of the best students give the right answers but are only using correctly memorized words [ 
                               … 
                            ] Many students have not developed an appropriate understanding of fundamental concepts from the beginning of their studies, and this shortcoming can interfere with subsequent learning. ( National Research Council, 1997  p. 27) 
                       Just because subsequent learning may be strongly affected by early formation of misconceptions, the sooner they are identified, the better.

The development of “misconceptions-aware” intelligent tutoring systems (ITSs) has been one of the ambitions for many of the approaches to computerized assessment of knowledge and learning. The different attempts to model misconceptions in an ITS can be roughly grouped into three distinct, though related, approaches: (1) cognitive tutors, (2) information systems, and (3) Bayesian networks. The first two are deterministic, whereas the latter is probabilistic.

Cognitive tutors ( Mitrovic, Koedinger, & Martin, 2003 ) represent skills and misconceptions by production rules of the general form “if  
                         X 
                       is matched by  
                         Y 
                       then transform  
                         Y 
                       into  
                         Z 
                      ”. An attempt to solve a problem is described as the repeated application of production rules until a solution or a failure is encountered. These production rules can either be correct (“expert rules”) or incorrect (“buggy rules”, e.g.,  Brown & Burton, 1978 ). Cognitive tutors assume that the process of problem solution is observable. However, in realistic situations this is not always the case. Rather, the solution process must be inferred from the observed response of the student.

The so far unique attempt to extend knowledge space theory to the modeling of misconceptions was developed by  Lukas (1997) . His extension is based on the notion of an information system introduced by  Scott (1982)  in computer science. The approach followed by Lukas separates a competence level from a performance level. At the competence level the student’s “competence state” is described as a set of skills (corresponding to the expert rules mentioned above) and misconceptions ( 
                         = 
                       buggy rules). At the performance level, the usual dichotomous item format (correct/incorrect) is generalized to polytomous response categories, some of which are obtainable by applying expert rules, whereas others are obtained by buggy rules (or mixtures of expert rules and buggy). The relationship between the two levels is only sketched by Lukas and it will be further generalized and refined in this article.

In the Bayesian networks (BNs) framework ( Conati, Gertner, & VanLehn, 2002; Mislevy & Gitomer, 1995; VanLehn et al., 2005 ), skills, misconceptions and items are different types of nodes of a directed graph. The nodes are linked together by edges representing conditional probabilities. When such a network is established for a certain domain of knowledge, Bayesian reasoning can be applied for inferring skills or misconceptions (which are latent and unobservable) from the observed responses to the items. A critical issue of the BN approach is that the conditional probabilities represented by the edges of the graph are unknown and their estimation from data is not trivial. Simplifying assumptions are sometimes used, that give rise to probabilistic models like the deterministic input noisy AND-gate (DINA,  Haertel, 1984, 1989, 1990 ) model or the deterministic input OR-gate (DINO,  Templin & Henson, 2006 ) model developed within the framework of cognitive diagnostic models (CDMs). All these models are based on the notion of a  
                         Q 
                      -matrix, a binary matrix used for establishing what attributes or skills in a set  
                         S 
                       are required for solving the items in a set  
                         Q 
                      . However, as recently pointed out by  Heller, Stefanutti, Anselmi, and Robusto (2015, 2016) , often such models are not identifiable, with the consequence that their assessment of the skills of an individual may fail to be unique. Other CDM approaches for modeling misconceptions and skills or handling multiple choice data exist. For example, the Simultaneously Identifying Skills and Misconceptions model by  Kuo, Chen, and de la Torre (2018)  measures skills and misconceptions in dichotomous data through combining the DINA and the Bug-DINO models.

More similar to the here presented approach, are the ones belonging to the family of Generalized Diagnostic Classification Models for Multiple Choice option-based scoring (GDCM-MC), presented by  DiBello, Henson, and Stout (2015) . This approach uses Multiple Choice data and models both skills and misconceptions. An extended non-binary  
                         Q 
                      -matrix is used to map skills and misconceptions to response categories of items. The GDCM-MC family can be seen as a generalization of almost every CDM. The different models are distinguished by their so-called kernel functions, which model the cognitive solution path. DiBello et al. show that the GDCM-MC cannot only describe solving by knowing and guessing behavior, but also a hybrid of both. For example, the multiple choice deterministic-input, noisy “and” gate (MC-DINA) model ( de La Torre, 2009 ) uses an extended  
                         Q 
                      -matrix to map skills to response categories of items. Even if it refers only to the possession or lack of skills and not to misconceptions, the approach of  de La Torre (2009)  is related to the here presented model. The MC-DINA assumes implicitly ordered response categories for classifying students into groups through their skills.

The present article is an attempt of collecting the essential and fundamental elements of the three theoretical strands sketched above into a unified formal approach for the modeling of individual skills and misconceptions. The approach is divided into a deterministic and a probabilistic part that both are based on the notion of a “polytomous skill map” (PSM). By mapping individual item responses to subsets of skills and misconceptions, the PSM provides the link between the competence level of the skills/misconceptions to the performance level of the responses to the items.

In the deterministic part, given a certain field of knowledge, three main collections are defined: (i) a set of problems (or items), (ii) a set of responses to the problems, and (iii) a set of rules. The collection of rules is further partitioned into skills (i.e., expert rules) and misconceptions (i.e., buggy rules). Relationships are established among the rules on the one side, and between the rules and the problems on the other side.

In general, skills may not be independent of one another. For instance, in the domain of fractions arithmetic, consider the two skills ( 
                         s 
                      ) “reduce a fraction” and ( 
                         t 
                      ) “find a common denominator”. It is plausible to assume that  
                         s 
                       is never learned before  
                         t 
                      , because an application of  
                         s 
                       in a specific fraction problem requires the application of  
                         t 
                       to the same problem. The binary relation better representing the condition “ 
                         s 
                       cannot be learned before  
                         t 
                      ” is a quasi-order (i.e., a reflexive and transitive relation) on the set of rules. Such a quasi-order is named a surmise relation on the set of rules.

A second form of dependence among the rules is consistency. When dealing with misconceptions, inconsistent combinations of rules may occur. To give an example, in solving an algebraic problem like “

                            2 
                            
                               ( 
                               a 
                               + 
                               b 
                               ) 
                            
                            = 
                            2 
                            a 
                            + 
                            2 
                            b 
                         
                      ” of multiplication. It seems highly reasonable that a single individual cannot hold both rules at the same time. That is, the set of the two turns out to be an “inconsistent” set of rules. Following the theoretical framework provided by knowledge space theory, the notion of a “competence state” is used for referring to the collection of all those rules used by an individual for solving problems in a certain domain (e.g., arithmetics). Such a collection could eventually be formed by a mixture of skills and misconceptions, but in a way that never results into an inconsistent set of rules.

Following  Lukas ’ ( 1997 ) approach, two logically different types of dependence among rules are considered in the proposed framework: “precedence” and “consistency”. A competence state is a set of rules that respects both types of dependence. There is, however, an important departure from that approach. The type of formalism used by Lukas, namely information systems, is a valid means for representing logical implication and consistency in a set of “tokens of information” (see, e.g.,  Davey & Priestley, 2002 ). It is shown here that only a special subfamily of the information systems can adequately represent a precedence relation among rules as it is usually conceived in knowledge space theory, that is a surmise relation. Such a special type of information systems are given the name of “quasi-ordinal systems of cognitive rules”. They are capable of representing both precedence (due to learning) and inconsistency among rules (due to misunderstanding). It is shown that the family of such special information systems is in a bijective correspondence with the family of the competence structures that are closed under intersection and “consistent unions”.

In the deterministic part, incorrect responses can only be interpreted as effects of misconceptions, as random error does not exist. This is not a very realistic situation. Therefore a probabilistic approach is needed, where careless errors might occur that lead to incorrect responses just as misconceptions do.

The probabilistic part of the proposed theory consists of a polytomous extension of the competence based local independence model (CBLIM;  Heller et al., 2015, 2016 ), named the “polytomous competence based local independence model” (PCBLIM). The CBLIM applies to dichotomous accuracy data. An appropriate data set for the CBLIM is a collection of response patterns, where each response pattern specifies, for each item in a set  
                         Q 
                      , if it was correctly solved by a given student or not. In the CBLIM, there is no way to distinguish among different incorrect answers. The polytomous extension proposed in this article admits items with more than two answer alternatives (polytomous items). The set of answer alternatives is unordered and it usually consists of a single correct answer plus an arbitrary number of incorrect alternatives. Like the CBLIM, the PCBLIM accounts for possible careless errors and lucky guesses in the individual responses. Unlike the CBLIM, the proposed model can separate systematic error due to misconceptions from random error due to carelessness or inattention.

In this section, a brief overview of knowledge space theory (KST,  Doignon & Falmagne, 1985, 1999; Falmagne & Doignon, 2011 ) is given. KST has been developed for the efficient assessment of knowledge and learning. It bears on two components; the former is a deterministic component, in which the relations among problems are specified through a theoretical structure. The latter is a probabilistic component, in which the deterministically specified theoretical structure is empirically validated by using a probabilistic model estimated on a data set. Sections  2.1 and 2.2  introduce the deterministic and the probabilistic parts of KST, respectively.

In knowledge space theory (KST,  Doignon & Falmagne, 1985, 1999; Falmagne & Doignon, 2011 ), the individual knowledge is represented by a  knowledge state  
                         
                            K 
                         . The state  
                            
                               K 
                               ⊆ 
                               Q 
                            
                          is the subset of problems, belonging to a particular domain  
                            Q 
                         , that a student masters. The collection of all the knowledge states existing in a population of students forms a  knowledge structure  ( 
                            
                               Q 
                               , 
                               K 
                            
                         ). The knowledge structure reflects the logical or pedagogical relations (e.g., prerequisites) among the problems in  
                            Q 
                         , and it delineates what a student is ready to learn next, given a specific knowledge state.

Competence-based KST (CbKST  Doignon, 1994; Düntsch & Gediga, 1995; Falmagne, Koppen, Villano, Doignon, & Johanessen, 1990; Gediga & Düntsch, 2002; Heller et al., 2015; Heller, Ünlü, & Albert, 2013; Korossy, 1993, 1997, 1999 ) extends the theory by a  competence level , describing the set  
                            S 
                          of skills involved in the solutions to the problems in  
                            Q

                               ⊆ 
                               S

                            s

                               ,

It is worth noticing that the problem function might not be injective, meaning that the same knowledge state could be delineated by more than one competence state ( Heller et al., 2015 ). Properties and conditions under which a problem function induces an injective correspondence between the knowledge and the competence states have not been studied yet.

In KST, the most studied and used probabilistic model is the  basic local independence model  (BLIM;  de Chiusole, Stefanutti, Anselmi, & Robusto, 2013; Falmagne & Doignon, 1988a, 1988b; Heller & Wickelmaier, 2013; Stefanutti & Robusto, 2009 ). It allows for an empirical validation of knowledge structures.

                                           C 
                                        
                                     
                                     
                                        
                                           π 
                                        
                                        
                                           C 
                                        
                                     
                                     
                                        ( 
                                        C 
                                        ) 
                                     
                                     , 
                                  
                               
                            
                         where  
                            
                               P 
                               
                                  
                                     R 
                                     ∣ 
                                     C 
                                  
                               
                               = 
                               P 
                               
                                  
                                     R 
                                     ∣ 
                                     p 
                                     
                                        ( 
                                        C 
                                        ) 
                                     
                                  
                               
                            
                          for all  
                            
                               C 
                               ∈ 
                               C 
                            
                         . This last conditional probability is computed exactly as Eq.  (4)  of the BLIM.

As mentioned above, if the problem function  
                            p 
                          is not bijective, the CBLIM is not identifiable, even if the BLIM defined on  
                            K 
                          is identifiable (see Corollary 1 and Proposition 8 in  Heller et al., 2015 ). Whereas, if the BLIM is identifiable and the problem function  
                            p 
                          is a bijection, the CBLIM is identifiable and it can be used for the empirical validation of competence structures.

Information systems were introduced by  Scott (1982) . A complete treatment of them can be found, for instance, in  Davey and Priestley (2002) . An  information system  is a triple  
                         
                            S 
                            = 
                            
                               〈 
                               S 
                               , 
                               C 
                               , 
                               ⊢ 
                               〉 
                            
                         
                       where:  
                         S 
                       is a set (of information units);  
                         C 
                       is a collection of finite subsets of  
                         S 
                       (the  consistent sets ) satisfying:

(IS1) 
                         
                            
                               
                                  
                                     
                                        { 
                                        a 
                                        } 
                                     
                                     ∈ 
                                     C 
                                  
                                for all  
                                  
                                     a 
                                     ∈ 
                                     S

                                     A 
                                     ∈ 
                                     C 
                                     ⟹ 
                                     B 
                                     ∈ 
                                     C 
                                  
                                for all  
                                  
                                     B

                                     A 
                                  
                               ;

{ 
                                        a

                                for all  
                                  
                                     a

                                     ⟹

                                     C 
                                  
                                for all

By Conditions  (IS1)  and  (IS2) , every singleton set is consistent, and every subset of a consistent set is consistent. Conditions  (IS3) ,  (IS4) , and  (IS5)  provide generalized versions of reflexivity and transitivity, and assure that the application of the relation  
                         ⊢ 
                       cannot lead to inconsistencies. A set  
                         
                            E 
                            ⊆ 
                            S 
                         
                       of information units is called an  element of  
                      
                         S 
                      , if:

(1) 
                            
                               
                                  E 
                                is consistent, which means  
                                  
                                     B 
                                     ∈ 
                                     C

                                     B

                                     B 
                                     ⊢ 
                                     a

                                     E

E 
                                is consistent, which means  
                                  
                                     B

                                     B 
                                     ⊆ 
                                     E

The collection  
                         E 
                       of all the elements of the information system  
                         S 
                       is an algebraic  
                         ⋂ 
                      –structure (i.e., a family of subsets of  
                         S 
                      , closed under intersection and directed unions). It is well known that information systems and algebraic  
                         ⋂

                            A 
                            , 
                            B

                            S

                            B

                            S

                                     ⋃ 
                                     E

                                     =

                                        F 
                                        ∈

Let  
                         δ 
                       denote a mapping such that, given any information system  
                         
                            ( 
                            S 
                            , 
                            C 
                            , 
                            ⊢ 
                            ) 
                         
                      ,  
                         
                            δ 
                            
                               ( 
                               S 
                               , 
                               C 
                               , 
                               ⊢ 
                               ) 
                            
                         
                       is the algebraic  
                         ⋂ 
                      -structure obtained by collecting all the elements (consistent and closed subsets of  
                         S 
                      ) of the information system. On the other side, given an algebraic  
                         ⋂ 
                      -structure  
                         E 
                      , let  
                         ρ 
                       denote a mapping such that  
                         
                            ρ 
                            
                               ( 
                               E 
                               ) 
                            
                         
                       is the information system obtained through an application of rules (i)–(iii) to  
                         E 
                      . Then the two mappings  
                         δ 
                       and  
                         ρ 
                       are mutually inverse bijective correspondences between the collection of all the information systems for the set  
                         S 
                      , and all the algebraic  
                         ⋂ 
                      -structures on  
                         S 
                      .

A polytomous skill map (PSM) is a generalization of a skill map ( Doignon, 1994 ) to items having more than two response categories. 
                         1 
                       
                      
                         1 
                         A comprehensive list of symbols and concepts defined in this and next sections can be found in Section  9 . 
                       Let  
                         Q 
                       be a set of problems, which are polytomous in the following sense: for every  
                         
                            q 
                            ∈ 
                            Q 
                         
                       we do not only consider if the problem was correctly solved or not, but we want to consider (some of) the specific ways of failing it. Thus, we distinguish a set  
                         V 
                       of  response categories , and a binary relation  
                         
                            A 
                            ⊆ 
                            Q 
                            × 
                            V 
                         
                      , such that, for  
                         
                            q 
                            ∈ 
                            Q 
                         
                       and  
                         
                            v 
                            ∈ 
                            V 
                         
                      , the pair  
                         
                            ( 
                            q 
                            , 
                            v 
                            ) 
                         
                       belongs to  
                         A 
                       whenever  
                         v 
                       is a response category for problem  
                         q 
                      . The collection of all the response categories for a given item  
                         
                            q 
                            ∈ 
                            Q 
                         
                       is denoted  
                         
                            
                               
                                  A 
                                  
                                     ( 
                                     q 
                                     ) 
                                  
                                  = 
                                  
                                     { 
                                     v 
                                     ∈ 
                                     V 
                                     : 
                                     q 
                                     A 
                                     v 
                                     } 
                                  
                                  . 
                               
                            
                         
                      At least one (usually, exactly one) of the response categories in  
                         
                            A 
                            
                               ( 
                               q 
                               ) 
                            
                         
                       is further classified as “correct”, whereas all the remaining ones are classified as “incorrect”. Let  
                         
                            
                               
                                  A 
                               
                               
                                  + 
                               
                            
                            ⊆ 
                            A 
                         
                       be the collection of all those pairs  
                         
                            
                               ( 
                               q 
                               , 
                               v 
                               ) 
                            
                            ∈ 
                            A 
                         
                       such that response category  
                         v 
                       is classified as “correct” for item  
                         q 
                      . Then,  
                         
                            
                               
                                  A 
                               
                               
                                  + 
                               
                            
                            
                               ( 
                               q 
                               ) 
                            
                            = 
                            
                               { 
                               v 
                               ∈ 
                               V 
                               : 
                               q 
                               
                                  
                                     A 
                                  
                                  
                                     + 
                                  
                               
                               v 
                               } 
                            
                         
                       is the collection of all the correct response categories for  
                         q 
                      . It is assumed that  
                         
                            
                               
                                  A 
                               
                               
                                  + 
                               
                            
                            
                               ( 
                               q 
                               ) 
                            
                         
                       is a singleton, so that exactly one of the response categories for  
                         q 
                       is classified as “correct”.

We further assume that, for any given problem, each response category is obtained through the application of some set of  rules . Each of the rules can be either correct (in which case it is named a  skill , or  expert rule ) or incorrect (in which case it is named a  misconception  or  buggy rule ).

The next step consists of deriving the knowledge structure for this augmented skill map. In principle one could consider all possible subsets of the four rules in  
                            S 
                         . Then, for each subset, the collection of item–response pairs that are obtainable by that combination is a knowledge state. However, those subsets of  
                            S 
                          including  
                            
                               {

This idea of “consistency” reflects the assumption that, in every given moment, an individual either has the skill or a misconception of the skill, but not both. Then the collection  
                            
                               
                                  C 
                               
                               
                                  1

Having excluded the inconsistent combinations, every knowledge state can now be obtained as the set of item–response pairs produced by one of the competence states, as shown in  Table 3 .

As mentioned above and illustrated in  Example 1 , our generalized notion of a knowledge state is now a subset of item–response pairs. However, not every subset of  
                            A

                            q

                            q

A few remarks are in order, concerning this definition. In the first place, it admits that different items may have different sets of response categories. A special case is represented by those situations where  
                            
                               A 
                               = 
                               Q 
                               × 
                               V 
                            
                          (e.g., some multiple-choice tests). That is, each of the response categories in  
                            V 
                          is valid for each of the items in  
                            Q 
                         . In those situations the binary relation  
                            A 
                          is unnecessary and it can be omitted, so that the polytomous knowledge structure is represented by a triple  
                            
                               ( 
                               Q 
                               , 
                               V 
                               , 
                               K 
                               ) 
                            
                         .

Secondly, the full set  
                            A 
                          of item response pairs does not belong to  
                            K 
                         . This is because, for example, two pairs  
                            
                               ( 
                               q 
                               , 
                               
                                  
                                     v 
                                  
                                  
                                     1 
                                  
                               
                               ) 
                            
                          and  
                            
                               ( 
                               q 
                               , 
                               
                                  
                                     v 
                                  
                                  
                                     2 
                                  
                               
                               ) 
                            
                          assigning different response categories to the same item cannot belong to the same knowledge state. However, in the whole, every item response pair  
                            
                               
                                  ( 
                                  q 
                                  , 
                                  v 
                                  ) 
                               
                               ∈ 
                               A 
                            
                          must belong to some of the knowledge states in  
                            K 
                         , so that the union  
                            
                               ⋃ 
                               K 
                            
                          equals  
                            A 
                         .

                            a

                            c 
                         , because they do not predict any specific response categories for them.

Thus, if an item cannot be solved correctly from a knowledge state, and the response category for that item is unknown, then the state will not contain any item–response pair for that item. This is the reason why the function  
                            K 
                         , introduced in  Definition 1 , is partial.

As observed above, the competence states are consistent sets containing a mix of skills and misconceptions. Consistency prevents that a skill and one of its misconceptions belong to the competence state of the same individual. Since competence states must be consistent subsets of  
                            S 
                         , and this last contains both the skills and the misconceptions, the competence structure  
                            C 
                          is typically a strict subset of the power set  
                            
                               
                                  2 
                               
                               
                                  S 
                               
                            
                         . It seems natural that such a collection satisfies at least the following two properties: (1) every state in  
                            C 
                          containing exactly one rule is consistent and (2) every subset of a consistent set is itself consistent. The subfamily of  
                            
                               
                                  2 
                               
                               
                                  S 
                               
                            
                          that satisfies these two properties is a “space of consistency”.

The main subject of this section is the notion of a “polytomous skill map”, which is a generalization of the (dichotomous) concept of a skill map. Whereas in a dichotomous skill map, any combination of skills in  
                            S 
                          can, in principle, be a competence state, this is no longer true of polytomous skill maps. Only consistent subsets of  
                            S 
                          can eventually be competence states. Moreover, while dichotomous skill maps assign nonempty subsets of skills to items, polytomous skill maps assign nonempty  and consistent  subsets of rules to item–response pairs. The intuition behind such an assignment is that every response category to every item is produced by a given (consistent) subset of rules. However, certain restrictions are necessary. In the first place, the deterministic nature of the skill map model imposes that the response category provided by an individual (in a given competence state) to an item is unique. Secondly, there must exist an individual who is capable of providing each item in  
                            Q 
                          with a “correct” response category. All of this is formally defined as follows.

The subset of rules  
                                  
                                     
                                        
                                           
                                              
                                                 S 
                                              
                                              
                                                 + 
                                              
                                           
                                           = 
                                           ⋃ 
                                           
                                              { 
                                              φ 
                                              
                                                 ( 
                                                 q 
                                                 , 
                                                 v 
                                                 ) 
                                              
                                              : 
                                              
                                                 ( 
                                                 q 
                                                 , 
                                                 v 
                                                 ) 
                                              
                                              ∈ 
                                              
                                                 
                                                    A 
                                                 
                                                 
                                                    + 
                                                 
                                              
                                              } 
                                           
                                        
                                     
                                  
                               is named the  set of skills . When clear from the context, the PSM  
                                  
                                     ( 
                                     A 
                                     , 
                                     S 
                                     , 
                                     C 
                                     , 
                                     φ 
                                     ) 
                                  
                                will be denoted by  
                                  φ 
                                only.

The mapping  
                            φ 
                          is interpreted as: “if the rules in  
                            
                               φ 
                               
                                  ( 
                                  q 
                                  , 
                                  v 
                                  ) 
                               
                            
                          are jointly applied to item  
                            
                               q 
                               ∈ 
                               Q 
                            
                         , then response category  
                            
                               v 
                               ∈ 
                               A 
                               
                                  ( 
                                  q 
                                  ) 
                               
                            
                          is obtained”. Condition  (PSM1)  assures uniqueness of the response category: every two distinct categories of responses to the same question are produced by incompatible (and hence distinct) subsets of rules. Condition  (PSM2)  assures the existence of an individual who correctly solves all items in  
                            Q 
                          (see  Proposition 2 ). We notice that  (PSM2)  is equivalent to stating that  
                            
                               
                                  
                                     S 
                                  
                                  
                                     + 
                                  
                               
                               ⊆ 
                               C 
                            
                          for some competence state  
                            
                               C 
                               ∈ 
                               D 
                            
                         .

It should be observed that, in the definition of a PSM, it is admitted that the relevant set of competence states may be a strict subset of the space of consistency. This possibility reveals to be particularly useful in the next section when dependencies among skills are considered.

The dichotomous notion of a conjunctive problem function ( Doignon, 1994 ) can be easily generalized to the polytomous context.

In CbKST, dependencies among skills are modeled by specifying, as the competence structure, a strict subset  
                         
                            D 
                            ⊂ 
                            
                               
                                  2 
                               
                               
                                  S 
                               
                            
                         
                       of the power set of the skills  
                         S 
                      . When the competence structure is the whole power set, the skills are “independent” in the sense that any combination of skills is a possible competence state. On the other side, if the competence structure is a strict subset of  
                         
                            
                               2 
                            
                            
                               S 
                            
                         
                      , then some combinations of skills are not allowed anymore. This condition introduces specific dependencies among the skills. Most typical forms of dependence are those expressing prerequisite relations among the skills: “skill  
                         s 
                       is a prerequisite for skill  
                         t 
                       if  
                         t 
                       cannot be learned before  
                         s 
                      ”.

The space of consistency  
                         C 
                       of  Definition 2  is, in general, itself a strict subset of the power set  
                         
                            
                               2 
                            
                            
                               S 
                            
                         
                      . In fact, it incorporates specific dependencies among the rules in  
                         S 
                      . Such dependencies may be regarded as incompatibilities among rules. Besides incompatibilities there may be other forms of dependency among the skills, like the prerequisite relation mentioned above. For instance, it may be possible that three rules  
                         
                            s 
                            , 
                            t 
                            , 
                            u 
                         
                       in  
                         S 
                       are such that  
                         s 
                       is a prerequisite for  
                         t 
                      , which, in turn, is not compatible with  
                         u 
                      . To be general, the following definition of a competence structure is provided, where the assumption is dropped that the full set of rules  
                         S 
                       is a member of the structure (see the missing maximum in  Fig. 1 ).

Definition 5 
                         A  competence structure  is a pair  
                               
                                  ( 
                                  S 
                                  , 
                                  D 
                                  ) 
                               
                            , where  
                               S 
                             is a set (of rules) and  
                               D 
                             is a family of subsets of  
                               S 
                             containing at least the empty set and such that  
                               
                                  ⋃ 
                                  D 
                                  = 
                                  S 
                               
                            .

A  competence structure  is a pair  
                               
                                  ( 
                                  S 
                                  , 
                                  D 
                                  ) 
                               
                            , where  
                               S 
                             is a set (of rules) and  
                               D 
                             is a family of subsets of  
                               S 
                             containing at least the empty set and such that  
                               
                                  ⋃ 
                                  D 
                                  = 
                                  S 
                               
                            .

Quasi-orders (i.e., reflexive and transitive binary relations) have been the starting point of KST. They were introduced in the theory for representing the dependency relation among items and, later, that among skills. Let  
                            ≾ 
                          be a quasi-order on the set  
                            S 
                          of rules or skills, and give it the following interpretation: given any two rules  
                            
                               r 
                               , 
                               s 
                               ∈ 
                               S 
                            
                         , write  
                            
                               r 
                               ≾ 
                               s 
                            
                          if any individual mastering rule  
                            s 
                          also masters rule  
                            r 
                         .

An example could be, if a student knows how to reduce a fraction like, e.g.,  
                            
                               12 
                               ∕ 
                               16 
                            
                          then she also knows how to find a common divisor between two integer numbers (e.g.,  
                            4 
                          is a common divisor of  
                            
                               12 
                            
                          and  
                            
                               16 
                            
                         ), so that  
                            
                               r 
                               = 
                            
                          “finding a common divisor” is a  prerequisite  for  
                            
                               s 
                               = 
                            
                          “reducing a fraction”.

Concerning Lukas’ approach to the modeling of skills and misconceptions, the “entailment relation” used in information systems is more general than a quasi-order. In this section the special case of an information system  
                            
                               ( 
                               S 
                               , 
                               C 
                               , 
                               ⊢ 
                               ) 
                            
                          is examined in which the entailment relation  
                            ⊢ 
                          is, indeed, a quasi-order on the set  
                            S 
                          of rules. This special case could be regarded as a “quasi-ordinal information system”. However, given that the subject matter of the present article is “cognitive rules”, the label “quasi-ordinal system of cognitive rules” or, for short, “quasi-ordinal rule system” seems more adequate in the present context.

Definition 6

                                     , 
                                     C

                                     ≾ 
                                     )

                                  S 
                                is a set,  
                                  C 
                                is a space of consistency for  
                                  S 
                                (i.e., a family of finite subsets of  
                                  S 
                                satisfying conditions  (CON1)  and  (CON2) ), and  
                                  ≾ 
                                is a quasi-order satisfying 
                            
                               
                                  (RS1) 
                                  
                                     if  
                                           
                                              s 
                                              ∈ 
                                              S 
                                           
                                        , and  
                                           
                                              s 
                                              ≾ 
                                              t 
                                           
                                         for some  
                                           
                                              t 
                                              ∈ 
                                              C 
                                              ∈ 
                                              C 
                                           
                                         then  
                                           
                                              C 
                                              ∪ 
                                              
                                                 { 
                                                 s 
                                                 } 
                                              
                                              ∈ 
                                              C 
                                           
                                        .

                                              ∈ 
                                              C 
                                           
                                         then  
                                           
                                              C 
                                              ∪ 
                                              
                                                 { 
                                                 s 
                                                 } 
                                              
                                              ∈ 
                                              C 
                                           
                                        .

What Property  (RS1)  states is that “if  
                            s 
                          and  
                            t 
                          are two rules and  
                            s 
                          is a prerequisite for  
                            t 
                         , then  
                            s 
                          must be compatible with all consistent subsets of rules that are compatible with  
                            t 
                         ”. This condition has a pedagogically sound consequence: if  
                            s 
                          is a prerequisite for  
                            t 
                         , then  
                            t 
                          cannot be compatible with any of the misconceptions of  
                            s 
                         .

The following result provides the link between quasi-ordinal systems of cognitive rules and information systems.

The competence structures derived from quasi-ordinal rule systems come with a special property.

Definition 8 
                            A subfamily  
                                  
                                     F 
                                     ⊆ 
                                     
                                        
                                           2 
                                        
                                        
                                           S 
                                        
                                     
                                  
                                is said to be  closed under consistent unions  if  
                                  
                                     G 
                                     ⊆ 
                                     F 
                                  
                               , and  
                                  
                                     ⋃ 
                                     G 
                                     ⊆ 
                                     H 
                                     ∈ 
                                     F 
                                  
                                imply  
                                  
                                     ⋃ 
                                     G 
                                     ∈ 
                                     F 
                                  
                               .

A subfamily  
                                  
                                     F 
                                     ⊆ 
                                     
                                        
                                           2 
                                        
                                        
                                           S 
                                        
                                     
                                  
                                is said to be  closed under consistent unions  if  
                                  
                                     G 
                                     ⊆ 
                                     F 
                                  
                               , and  
                                  
                                     ⋃ 
                                     G 
                                     ⊆ 
                                     H 
                                     ∈ 
                                     F 
                                  
                                imply  
                                  
                                     ⋃ 
                                     G 
                                     ∈ 
                                     F 
                                  
                               .

If  
                            S 
                          is finite then a simple induction argument shows that  
                            F 
                          is closed under consistent unions if and only if  
                            
                               A 
                               , 
                               B 
                               , 
                               C 
                               ∈ 
                               F 
                            
                          and  
                            
                               A 
                               ∪ 
                               B 
                               ⊆ 
                               C 
                            
                          imply  
                            
                               A 
                               ∪ 
                               B 
                               ∈ 
                               F 
                            
                         . The choice of the term “consistent unions” has the following reason. If  
                            F 
                          is the competence structure derived from an information system, then the union of any two states  
                            A 
                          and  
                            B 
                          in  
                            F 
                          can be in the structure only if the union itself is a consistent set, that is  
                            
                               A 
                               ∪ 
                               B 
                            
                          must be a “consistent union”.

Proposition 6 
                            
                               The competence structure derived from a quasi-ordinal rule system is closed under intersection and consistent unions.

The competence structure derived from a quasi-ordinal rule system is closed under intersection and consistent unions.

Proof 
                            By  Proposition 5 , a quasi-ordinal rule system  
                                  
                                     ( 
                                     S 
                                     , 
                                     C 
                                     , 
                                     ≾ 
                                     ) 
                                  
                                is an information system, hence the competence structure  
                                  D 
                                of the system is closed under intersection. Thus we only have to show that it is closed under consistent unions. Let  
                                  
                                     G 
                                     ⊆ 
                                     D 
                                  
                                and  
                                  
                                     H 
                                     ∈ 
                                     D 
                                  
                                be such that  
                                  
                                     ⋃ 
                                     G 
                                     ⊆ 
                                     H 
                                  
                               . Consistency: Consider any finite subset  
                                  
                                     C 
                                     ∈ 
                                     
                                        
                                           2 
                                        
                                        
                                           Q 
                                        
                                     
                                  
                                with  
                                  
                                     C 
                                     ⊆ 
                                     ⋃ 
                                     G 
                                  
                               . Then  
                                  
                                     C 
                                     ⊆ 
                                     H 
                                  
                                and, since  
                                  H 
                                is consistent and  
                                  C 
                                is finite, it holds that  
                                  
                                     C 
                                     ∈ 
                                     C 
                                  
                               . Thus,  
                                  
                                     ⋃ 
                                     G 
                                  
                                must be consistent too. Closure: Suppose now  
                                  
                                     s 
                                     ≾ 
                                     t 
                                  
                                for some  
                                  
                                     t 
                                     ∈ 
                                     ⋃ 
                                     G 
                                  
                               . Then there is  
                                  
                                     G 
                                     ∈ 
                                     G 
                                  
                                with  
                                  
                                     t 
                                     ∈ 
                                     G 
                                  
                               . But from  
                                  
                                     s 
                                     ≾ 
                                     t 
                                     ∈ 
                                     G 
                                  
                                and the fact that  
                                  G 
                                is closed it follows that  
                                  
                                     s 
                                     ∈ 
                                     G 
                                  
                                and, hence  
                                  
                                     s 
                                     ∈ 
                                     ⋃ 
                                     G 
                                  
                               . Thus  
                                  
                                     ⋃ 
                                     G 
                                  
                                is closed. Since it is both consistent and closed, it must belong to  
                                  D 
                               .  □

While all quasi-ordinal rule systems are information systems, the converse does not hold in general, as the following example shows. As a consequence, the competence structure of the information system does not need to be closed under consistent unions.

Example 4

As argued in Section  3 , it is a well-established fact that the information systems for a given nonempty set  
                            S 
                          are in a one-to-one correspondence with the algebraic  
                            ⋂ 
                         -structures on the same set. A similar result can be obtained for the rule systems and the competence structures closed under intersection and consistent unions.

Let  
                            R 
                          denote the family of the essentially finite systems of cognitive rules for the set  
                            S 
                         , and  
                            ℭ 
                          denote the collection of all the finite competence structures on  
                            S 
                          that are closed under both intersection and consistent unions. Moreover, considering again the two mappings  
                            δ 
                          and  
                            ρ 
                          defined at the end of Section  3 , let  
                            
                               
                                  δ 
                               
                               
                                  ∗

                            δ 
                          to the family  
                            R 
                         , and  
                            
                               
                                  ρ 
                               
                               
                                  ∗ 
                               
                            
                          denote the restriction of  
                            ρ 
                          to the collection  
                            ℭ 
                         . Then the following result obtains.

Proposition 7

The competence structure and the polytomous knowledge structures are deterministic models that need to be empirically validated. Suppose that some data set is available containing the answers of a sample of individuals to a set  
                         Q 
                       of items. Suppose further that a competence structure  
                         C 
                       and the corresponding polytomous knowledge structure  
                         K 
                       are available. A  probabilistic model  is needed for the empirical validation of  
                         C 
                       and  
                         K 
                      , as well as for the diagnosis of skills and misconceptions in individual learners.

The BLIM and the CBLIM are suitable for the empirical validation of knowledge and competence structures built on dichotomous items. In such models, the only distinctive feature of a response is in terms of accuracy. The response can only be either “correct” or “incorrect”. In this sense the data are dichotomous. The models can be generalized to polytomous structures in the sense that, for each item, more than two response categories are distinguished, each of which is further classified as “correct” or “incorrect”. Moreover, the number of response categories may vary from item to item. In this section we propose a polytomous extension of the CBLIM, that is called the  polytomous competence based local independence model  (PCBLIM).

In the BLIM, the response pattern of an individual is defined as the subset of items in  
                         Q 
                       for which a correct answer is provided. In the polytomous framework the response pattern is defined as a mapping  
                         
                            R 
                            : 
                            Q 
                            → 
                            V 
                            ∪ 
                            
                               { 
                               0 
                               } 
                            
                         
                      , where “ 
                         0 
                      ” is a special symbol denoting the response category collecting all those incorrect responses to the items in  
                         Q 
                       that do not belong to any of the response categories in  
                         V 
                      . Thus, the definition of a response pattern differs from that of a knowledge state for the fact that the former is always a total function, whereas the latter is partial. To relate this definition to the dichotomous case, one would have  
                         
                            V 
                            = 
                            
                               { 
                               1 
                               } 
                            
                         
                       so that  
                         R 
                       maps  
                         Q 
                       to  
                         
                            { 
                            0 
                            , 
                            1 
                            } 
                         
                      , becoming the membership function of the set containing all and only the items receiving a correct response.

In this section we provide an example of how the deterministic extension of KST to misconceptions can be empirically validated by applying the PCBLIM to a real data set. It is worth noticing that this example is not intended as a general validation for modeling misconceptions. Instead, the way for estimating the parameters of the PCBLIM and for conducting a goodness of fit test for it are presented and discussed.

Three skill maps were defined for a domain  
                         Q 
                       of five items about combinatorics and probability theory. The polytomous skill map  
                         
                            
                               φ 
                            
                            
                               0 
                            
                         
                       is a standard CbKST skill map that does not contain any misconception. In the two polytomous skill maps  
                         
                            
                               φ 
                            
                            
                               1 
                            
                         
                       and  
                         
                            
                               φ 
                            
                            
                               2 
                            
                         
                      , we hypothesized one misconception and two misconceptions for each skill, respectively. In general, it is possible to consider many other skills and many other misconceptions for the same domain, and as the number of misconceptions per skill increases, the amount of information about the students’ knowledge also increases.

The data were collected from 357 psychology students of the University of Padua who were attending the Psychometrics course for the academic year 2016–2017. The students answered to 28 open-answer items about probability theory and combinatorics, through a computer-based testing procedure. The items were administered in a random order, in a two-hour session. Since the aim of this empirical application is to give an example of the practical applicability of the earlier presented approach, a subset of five items was selected for the analyses. Thus, a domain  
                            
                               Q 
                               = 
                               
                                  { 
                                  
                                     
                                        q 
                                     
                                     
                                        1 
                                     
                                  
                                  , 
                                  
                                     
                                        q 
                                     
                                     
                                        2 
                                     
                                  
                                  , 
                                  
                                     
                                        q 
                                     
                                     
                                        3

                                        4 
                                     
                                  
                                  , 
                                  
                                     
                                        q 
                                     
                                     
                                        5 
                                     
                                  
                                  } 
                               
                            
                          was considered, with the following items:

(

                               A student has to choose 3 books from a list of 9. How many different options does she have? [84]

A student has to choose 3 books from a list of 9. How many different options does she have? [84]

A commission must be formed by 5 or more persons, chosen from a list of 7. In how many ways can the commission be composed? [29]

Flipping an unfair coin, if the probability of a head is

                                        2 
                                        ∕ 
                                        3 
                                     
                                  , which is the probability of observing the sequence “THHTH”? [0.033]

Flipping 9 times an unfair coin, if the probability of a head is
