                                        3 
                                     
                                  , which is the probability of observing at least 8 heads? [0.143]

The data was stored in anonymized form. The raw data matrix consisted of the numerical answers given by the students to the items. This matrix was used in later analyses for recoding the data and for arranging them for the PCBLIM estimation. In total, 11% of the data were missing. Only the response patterns having more than the 50% of answers were considered, including only those patterns with at least three responses. The final sample size was 331 with 7% of missing responses. An adapted version of the maximum likelihood estimation procedure by  de Chiusole, Stefanutti, Anselmi, and Robusto (2015)  was used for the parameter fit; this procedure provides asymptotically unbiased estimates of the model parameters under the missing-at-random assumption.

Their interpretation is based on the assumption that the solution process decomposes into a number of “steps” (represented by the  skills ), all of which are necessary for solving the problem. A distinction is made between the situation (represented by

It is important to underline here that, in principle, both cases “miss a step” and “fail a step” could arise as consequences of a systematic failure or a careless error. Some might argue that they can be interpreted as misconceptions only if the failures occur systematically and repeatedly.

                         , respectively. Whenever the answer of a student to an item was incorrect and did not fit any of the response categories defined in

                                  0

                         , it was assigned to response category 0. The parameters of the BLIM and those of the PCBLIM were estimated by maximum likelihood via the expectation–maximization algorithm. Concerning the BLIM, the procedure was that described in  Stefanutti and Robusto (2009)  whereas, for the PCBLIM, the estimation algorithm was developed by the authors in MATLAB (the code is available upon request from the authors). This results in three different models: (M0) a BLIM based on  
                            
                               
                                  K 
                               
                               
                                  0 
                               
                            
                          estimated on the dichotomous data  
                            
                               
                                  D 
                               
                               
                                  0 
                               
                            
                         , (M1) a PCBLIM based on  
                            
                               
                                  K 
                               
                               
                                  1 
                               
                            
                          estimated on  
                            
                               
                                  D 
                               
                               
                                  1 
                               
                            
                         , and (M2) a PCBLIM based on  
                            
                               
                                  K 
                               
                               
                                  2 
                               
                            
                          estimated on  
                            
                               
                                  D 
                               
                               
                                  2 
                               
                            
                         . It should be observed that no standard statistical comparison among the three models (M0), (M1) and (M2) is possible, due to the fact that each of them was fitted to its own data set. Therefore, only absolute fit indexes were considered.

To test the models’ absolute goodness-of-fit, the Pearson chi-square statistic was used. A parametric bootstrap procedure over 500 replications was used for computing the  
                            p 
                         -value of the chi-square. This is necessary because the approximation to the asymptotic distribution of the Chi-square statistic lacks accuracy for large and sparse data matrices and this was the case of the present empirical application. Parametric bootstrap was performed in the following way: (i) The parameter estimates obtained by fitting the model to the data were used to generate 500 data sets of the same size of the observed sample ( 
                            
                               N 
                               = 
                               331 
                            
                         ); (ii) the model was fitted to each of the simulated data sets; and (iii) the proportion of replications in which the model obtained a chi-square greater than the observed one was the bootstrapped  
                            p 
                         -value.

Besides parameter estimation, the conditional probability  
                            
                               P 
                               
                                  
                                     K 
                                     ∣ 
                                     R 
                                  
                               
                            
                          was computed for each response pattern by an application of Bayes’ theorem, that is:

                                                 π 
                                              
                                              
                                                 K 
                                              
                                           
                                           
                                              ( 
                                              K 
                                              ) 
                                           
                                        
                                        
                                           P 
                                           
                                              ( 
                                              R 
                                              ) 
                                           
                                        
                                     
                                     , 
                                  
                               
                            
                         where  
                            
                               P 
                               
                                  ( 
                                  R 
                                  ) 
                               
                            
                          is computed by Eq.  (1)  for the BLIM (M0) and by Eq.  (11)  for the PCBLIM, that is, (M1) and (M2);  
                            
                               P 
                               
                                  
                                     R 
                                     ∣ 
                                     K 
                                  
                               
                            
                          is computed by Eq.  (10)  in the case of (M1) and (M2) and by Eq.  (4)  in the case of (M0);  
                            
                               
                                  
                                     π 
                                  
                                  
                                     K 
                                  
                               
                               
                                  ( 
                                  K 
                                  ) 
                               
                            
                          is estimated from the data, separately for (M0), (M1) and (M2).

Finally, for each model and for each student in the sample, the modal state was determined. It is computed by taking the maximum of the conditional probability  
                            
                               P 
                               
                                  
                                     K 
                                     ∣ 
                                     R 
                                  
                               
                            
                          among all states. In this way, for each student, three different modal states were obtained.

The bootstrapped  
                            p 
                         -value of the two PCBLIMs (M1) and (M2) was .05 and .11, respectively. That of the BLIM (M0) was less than .01. Considering a significance level of  
                            
                               α 
                               = 
                               . 
                               10 
                            
                          for model validation, only models (M2) is consistent with the data.

It is worth noticing that both approaches are more informative than the dichotomous one since they were also able to find what specific buggy rules the students applied to problems. Moreover, the approach with six misconceptions is more informative than the one with three misconceptions. In particular, the former approach establishes that all the 11 students in the example at hand  performed all the necessary steps  for solving each of the five items, although some steps were performed in an incorrect way (wrong formulas). Based on model (M1) it is not possible to separate the two types of misconceptions, therefore missing a step or performing it incorrectly are treated as the same type of error.

The important role of misconceptions for knowledge acquisition and learning is reflected by several approaches to computerized assessment of knowledge and learning (e.g., cognitive tutors, information systems, Bayesian networks). Knowledge space theory has been developed for a formative assessment of knowledge and learning, and it is one of the most promising approaches for theory-driven development of intelligent tutoring systems. However, so far, only one attempt was made to generalize KST to the assessment of misconceptions ( Lukas, 1997 ). In the present article, Lukas’ approach was further generalized and refined.

Our approach is divided into a deterministic and a probabilistic part. In the deterministic part, the  polytomous skill map  represents the cornerstone, by mapping individual item responses to subsets of skills and misconceptions. The PSM thereby provides the link between the competence level of the skills and misconceptions to the performance level of the correct or incorrect responses to the items. Three main collections are introduced for specifying the deterministic part: (i) a set of problems, (ii) a set of responses to the problems, and (iii) a set of rules partitioned into skills (expert rules) and misconceptions (buggy rules). The relationships established among rules (i.e., precedence and consistency relations) on the one side, and among rules and problems on the other side (i.e., the skill map) allow to delineate a  polytomous knowledge structure . In the polytomous knowledge structure, each problem is characterized by two or more response categories, that is: the one corresponding to the correct answer (resulting from the application of one or more skills); the one corresponding to wrong answers due to unknown reasons, and eventually those corresponding to the answers resulting from the application of skills and at least one misconception. The probabilistic part consists in an extension of the CBLIM, that has been coined  polytomous competence based local independence model  (PCBLIM).

In Section  7 , we provided an example to show how the developed deterministic and the probabilistic approaches apply in the real word. Two different polytomous skill maps containing both skills and misconceptions were drawn for a domain about combinatorics and probability theory. We considered two polytomous skill maps for showing two different approaches for modeling misconceptions within the same domain. In the former approach, we predefined one misconception in addition to each skill, whereas two misconceptions per skill were defined in the latter. The PCBLIM based on both the skill maps was then applied to a real data set. Both models provided a good fit to the data, and as the number of misconceptions per skill increased, the amount of information about the students’ knowledge also increased.

Concerning future applications, it is possible to extend ITSs based on KST, like, for example, Stat-Knowlab ( https://stat-knowlab.unipd.it/ ,  Stefanutti & de Chiusole, 2017 ), to the assessment of students’ skills and misconceptions. From a practical perspective, the assessment of misconceptions will result in more fine-grained knowledge diagnostics, and, as a consequence, in an enhanced personalization of students’ learning. Indeed, the approach proposed in the present research not only enables to find the exact step of the solution in which the student fails, but also the reason why she failed.

Despite the important theoretical and practical results of this research, some limitations have to be underlined. The theoretical results described in the article are about the conjunctive model. This last is based on the assumption that an individual produces a response category  
                            v 
                          only if she knows all the rules in  
                            
                               φ 
                               
                                  ( 
                                  q 
                                  , 
                                  v 
                                  ) 
                               
                            
                         . If any one of them is not possessed, the response category  
                            v 
                          is not obtained. The main limitation of the conjunctive model is that, for every single response category there exists only one minimally sufficient subset of rules. In real situations, the same (correct or incorrect) response to a problem may be obtained in more than a single way. That is, there may be more than a single minimally sufficient subset of rules. This is the basic principle underlying a disjunctive model, which could be considered in future extensions of the theory.

Moreover, the probabilistic model proposed for the empirical validation of the polytomous structures (i.e., the PCBLIM) has a large number of free parameters. In particular, the number of the model parameters increases very fast as the number of items increases. In Section  7  we demonstrated how a reparameterization of the model can effectively reduce the degrees of freedom of the model and, thus, the complexity of their interpretation. Nevertheless, future research could aim for more parsimonious models with less complexity, that are still aware of misconceptions.

Finally, a crucial issue in KST is the specification of the correct knowledge structure for a given set of problems. In the theory-driven approach, the knowledge structure is specified with help of expert queries, task analyses and skill maps ( Doignon, 1994; Düntsch & Gediga, 1995; Heller et al., 2013; Lukas & Albert, 1993 ). In Section  4  we have shown how the skill map in CbKST is generalized to a polytomous skill map that includes misconceptions, and the examples shown in Section  7  are instances of it. In contrast, in the data-driven approach, the knowledge structure is derived from a large set of data (e.g.,  de Chiusole, Stefanutti, & Spoto, 2017; Robusto & Stefanutti, 2014; Schrepp, 1999; Spoto, Stefanutti, & Vidotto, 2016 ).

The data-driven approaches pose specific restrictions on the knowledge structure ( Sargin & Ünlü, 2009; Schrepp, 2003 ) and the data; for example, if performance is contaminated by lucky guesses and careless errors, data-driven identification of a knowledge structure is more difficult ( Robusto & Stefanutti, 2014 , see also  Spoto, Stefanutti, & Vidotto, 2012 ). Because the skill map is, in general, not bijective, purely data-driven specification of the competence structure seems to be infeasible; this problem is amplified when the competence structure includes misconceptions. In other words, ITSs based on CbKST are limited to theoretically predefined skills and misconceptions. However, if many instances of the same incorrect response occur, an ITS might at least indicate where to start the search for the underlying misconception(s) by presenting problems in which selected skills have been removed.
